style="background-color:#f9f9f9;border:1px solid #aaa;color:#000;width: 22em;">\n<tbody><tr>\n<td style="width:1px"></td>\n<td class="mbox-text plainlist" style="vertical-align:middle; font-size:95%;"><b>This article contains <a href="/wiki/Help:Special_characters" title="Help:Special characters">special characters</a>.</b> Without proper <a href="/wiki/Help:Special_characters" title="Help:Special characters">rendering support</a>, you may see <a href="/wiki/Specials_(Unicode_block)#Replacement_character" title="Specials (Unicode block)">question marks, boxes, or other symbols</a>.</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><b>Zeus</b> (<span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="/zj/: \'Z\' in \'Zeus\'">zj</span><span title="/u&#720;/: \'oo\' in \'goose\'">u&#720;</span><span title="\'s\' in \'sigh\'">s</span></span>/</a></span></span><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[3]</a></sup> or <span class="nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="\'z\' in \'zoom\'">z</span><span title="/u&#720;/: \'oo\' in \'goose\'">u&#720;</span><span title="\'s\' in \'sigh\'">s</span></span>/</a></span></span>;<a href="/wiki/Greek_language" title="Greek language">Greek</a>: <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">&#918;&#949;&#973;&#962;</span>, <i>Ze&#250;s</i> <small></small><span class="IPA" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Greek" title="Help:IPA/Greek">[zde&#468;&#815;s]</a></span>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[4]</a></sup> is the <a href="/wiki/Sky_father" title="Sky father">sky</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Thunder_god" title="Thunder god">thunder god</a> in <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greek_religion" title="Ancient Greek religion">ancient Greek religion</a>, who rules as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_of_the_gods" title="King of the gods">king of the gods</a> of <a href="/wiki/Mount_Olympus" title="Mount Olympus">Mount Olympus</a>. His name is <a href="/wiki/Cognate" title="Cognate">cognate</a> with the first element of his <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Roman_religion" title="Ancient Roman religion">Roman</a> <a href="/wiki/Interpretatio_graeca" title="Interpretatio graeca">equivalent</a> <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_(mythology)" title="Jupiter (mythology)">Jupiter</a>. His mythologies and powers are similar, though not identical, to those of Indo-European deities such as <a href="/wiki/Indra" title="Indra">Indra</a>, <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_(mythology)" title="Jupiter (mythology)">Jupiter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Perk%C5%ABnas" title="Perk&#363;nas">Perk&#363;nas</a>, <a href="/wiki/Perun" title="Perun">Perun</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Thor" title="Thor">Thor</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Berry1996p20_5-0"><a href="#cite_note-Berry1996p20-5">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-6"><a href="#cite_note-6">[6]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-7"><a href="#cite_note-7">[7]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Zeus is the child of <a href="/wiki/Cronus" title="Cronus">Cronus</a> and <a href="/wiki/Rhea_(mythology)" title="Rhea (mythology)">Rhea</a>, the youngest of his siblings to be born, though sometimes reckoned the eldest as the others required disgorging from Cronus\'s stomach. In most traditions, he is married to <a href="/wiki/Hera" title="Hera">Hera</a>, by whom he is usually said to have fathered <a href="/wiki/Ares" title="Ares">Ares</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hebe_(mythology)" title="Hebe (mythology)">Hebe</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Hephaestus" title="Hephaestus">Hephaestus</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hamilton1942_8-0"><a href="#cite_note-Hamilton1942-8">[8]</a></sup> At the <a href="/wiki/Oracle" title="Oracle">oracle</a> of <a href="/wiki/Dodona" title="Dodona">Dodona</a>, his consort was said to be <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dione_(Titaness/Oceanid)" title="Dione (Titaness/Oceanid)">Dione</a>, by whom the <i><a href="/wiki/Iliad" title="Iliad">Iliad</a></i> states that he fathered <a href="/wiki/Aphrodite" title="Aphrodite">Aphrodite</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-aphrodite_11-0"><a href="#cite_note-aphrodite-11">[11]</a></sup> Zeus was also infamous for his erotic escapades. These resulted in many divine and heroic offspring, including <a href="/wiki/Athena" title="Athena">Athena</a>, <a href="/wiki/Apollo" title="Apollo">Apollo</a>, <a href="/wiki/Artemis" title="Artemis">Artemis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hermes" title="Hermes">Hermes</a>, <a href="/wiki/Persephone" title="Persephone">Persephone</a>, <a href="/wiki/Dionysus" title="Dionysus">Dionysus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Perseus" title="Perseus">Perseus</a>, <a href="/wiki/Heracles" title="Heracles">Heracles</a>, <a href="/wiki/Helen_of_Troy" title="Helen of Troy">Helen of Troy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Minos" title="Minos">Minos</a>, and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Muse" title="Muse">Muses</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Hamilton1942_8-1"><a href="#cite_note-Hamilton1942-8">[8]</a></sup>\n</p><p>He was respected as an <a href="/wiki/Sky_father" title="Sky father">allfather</a> who was chief of the gods<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[12]</a></sup> and assigned the others to their roles:<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[13]</a></sup> "Even the gods who are not his natural children address him as Father, and all the gods rise in his presence."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-14"><a href="#cite_note-14">[14]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-15"><a href="#cite_note-15">[15]</a></sup> He was <a href="/wiki/Interpretatio_graeca" title="Interpretatio graeca">equated</a> with many foreign <a href="/wiki/Weather_god" title="Weather god">weather gods</a>, permitting <a href="/wiki/Pausanias_(geographer)" title="Pausanias (geographer)">Pausanias</a> to observe "That Zeus is king in heaven is a saying common to all men".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-16"><a href="#cite_note-16">[16]</a></sup> Zeus\' symbols are the <a href="/wiki/Thunderbolt" title="Thunderbolt">thunderbolt</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eagle" title="Eagle">eagle</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bull_(mythology)" title="Bull (mythology)">bull</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Oak" title="Oak">oak</a>. In addition to his <a href="/wiki/Dyeus" title="Dyeus">Indo-European inheritance</a>, the classical "cloud-gatherer" (Greek: <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">&#925;&#949;&#966;&#949;&#955;&#951;&#947;&#949;&#961;&#941;&#964;&#945;</span>, <i>Nephel&#275;gereta</i>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-17"><a href="#cite_note-17">[17]</a></sup>  also derives certain iconographic traits from the cultures of the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Near_East" title="Ancient Near East">ancient Near East</a>, such as the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Scepter" title="Scepter">scepter</a>. Zeus is frequently depicted by Greek artists in one of two poses: standing, striding forward with a thunderbolt leveled in his raised right hand, or seated in majesty.\n</p>\n<div class="toc" id="toc"><input class="toctogglecheckbox" id="toctogglecheckbox" role="button" style="display:none" type="checkbox"/><div class="toctitle" dir="ltr" lang="en"><h2>Contents</h2><span class="toctogglespan"><label class="toctogglelabel" for="toctogglecheckbox"></label></span></div>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-1"><a href="#Name"><span class="tocnumber">1</span> <span class="toctext">Name</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-2"><a href="#Mythology"><span class="tocnumber">2</span> <span class="toctext">Mythology</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-3"><a href="#Birth"><span class="tocnumber">2.1</span> <span class="toctext">Birth</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-4"><a href="#Infancy"><span class="tocnumber">2.2</span> <span class="toctext">Infancy</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-5"><a href="#King_of_the_gods"><span class="tocnumber">2.3</span> <span class="toctext">King of the gods</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-6"><a href="#Conflicts_with_humans"><span class="tocnumber">2.4</span> <span class="toctext">Conflicts with humans</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-7"><a href="#In_the_Iliad"><span class="tocnumber">2.5</span> <span class="toctext">In the <i>Iliad</i></span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-8"><a href="#List_of_other_deeds"><span class="tocnumber">2.6</span> <span class="toctext">List of other deeds</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-9"><a href="#Family"><span class="tocnumber">3</span> <span class="toctext">Family</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-10"><a href="#Zeus_and_Hera"><span class="tocnumber">3.1</span> <span class="toctext">Zeus and Hera</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-11"><a href="#Transformation_of_Zeus"><span class="tocnumber">3.2</span> <span class="toctext">Transformation of Zeus</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-12"><a href="#Consorts_and_offspring"><span class="tocnumber">3.3</span> <span class="toctext">Consorts and offspring</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-13"><a href="#Roles_and_epithets"><span class="tocnumber">4</span> <span class="toctext">Roles and epithets</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-14"><a href="#Cults_of_Zeus"><span class="tocnumber">5</span> <span class="toctext">Cults of Zeus</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-15"><a href="#Panhellenic_cults"><span class="tocnumber">5.1</span> <span class="toctext">Panhellenic cults</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-16"><a href="#Zeus_Velchanos"><span class="tocnumber">5.1.1</span> <span class="toctext">Zeus Velchanos</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-17"><a href="#Zeus_Lykaios"><span class="tocnumber">5.1.2</span> <span class="toctext">Zeus Lykaios</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-18"><a href="#Additional_cults_of_Zeus"><span class="tocnumber">5.1.3</span> <span class="toctext">Additional cults of Zeus</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-19"><a href="#Non-panhellenic_cults"><span class="tocnumber">5.2</span> <span class="toctext">Non-panhellenic cults</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-2 tocsection-20"><a href="#Oracles_of_Zeus"><span class="tocnumber">5.3</span> <span class="toctext">Oracles of Zeus</span></a>\n<ul>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-21"><a href="#The_Oracle_at_Dodona"><span class="tocnumber">5.3.1</span> <span class="toctext">The Oracle at Dodona</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-3 tocsection-22"><a href="#The_Oracle_at_Siwa"><span class="tocnumber">5.3.2</span> <span class="toctext">The Oracle at Siwa</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n</ul>\n</li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-23"><a href="#Zeus_and_foreign_gods"><span class="tocnumber">6</span> <span class="toctext">Zeus and foreign gods</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-24"><a href="#Zeus_and_the_sun"><span class="tocnumber">7</span> <span class="toctext">Zeus and the sun</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-25"><a href="#Zeus_in_philosophy"><span class="tocnumber">8</span> <span class="toctext">Zeus in philosophy</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-26"><a href="#Zeus_in_the_Bible"><span class="tocnumber">9</span> <span class="toctext">Zeus in the Bible</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-27"><a href="#In_modern_culture"><span class="tocnumber">10</span> <span class="toctext">In modern culture</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-28"><a href="#Genealogy_of_the_Olympians"><span class="tocnumber">11</span> <span class="toctext">Genealogy of the Olympians</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-29"><a href="#Argive_genealogy"><span class="tocnumber">12</span> <span class="toctext">Argive genealogy</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-30"><a href="#See_also"><span class="tocnumber">13</span> <span class="toctext">See also</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-31"><a href="#Notes"><span class="tocnumber">14</span> <span class="toctext">Notes</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-32"><a href="#References"><span class="tocnumber">15</span> <span class="toctext">References</span></a></li>\n<li class="toclevel-1 tocsection-33"><a href="#External_links"><span class="tocnumber">16</span> <span class="toctext">External links</span></a></li>\n</ul>\n</div>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name">Name</span></h2>\n<p>The god\'s name in the nominative is <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">&#918;&#949;&#973;&#962;</span> (<i>Ze&#250;s</i>). It is inflected as follows: <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vocative" title="Vocative">vocative</a>: <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">&#918;&#949;&#8166;</span> (<i><i lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek language text">Ze&#251;</i></i>); <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Accusative" title="Accusative">accusative</a>: <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">&#916;&#943;&#945;</span> (<i lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek language text">D&#237;a</i>); <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Genitive" title="Genitive">genitive</a>: <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">&#916;&#953;&#972;&#962;</span> (<i lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek language text">Di&#243;s</i>); <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dative" title="Dative">dative</a>: <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">&#916;&#953;&#943;</span> (<i lang="grc-Latn" title="Ancient Greek language text">Di&#237;</i>). <a href="/wiki/Diogenes_La%C3%ABrtius" title="Diogenes La&#235;rtius">Diogenes La&#235;rtius</a> quotes <a href="/wiki/Pherecydes_of_Syros" title="Pherecydes of Syros">Pherecydes of Syros</a> as spelling the name, <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">&#918;&#940;&#962;</span>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-DioL1.11_18-0"><a href="#cite_note-DioL1.11-18">[18]</a></sup>\n</p><p><i>Zeus</i> is the Greek continuation of *<i><a href="/wiki/Dyeus" title="Dyeus"><span class="Unicode" lang="ine" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language">Di&#815;&#275;us</span></a>,</i> the name of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_religion" title="Proto-Indo-European religion">Proto-Indo-European</a> god of the daytime sky, also called *<i><span class="Unicode" lang="ine" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language">Dyeus ph<sub>2</sub>t&#275;r</span></i> ("Sky Father").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zeus_19-0"><a href="#cite_note-Zeus-19">[19]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-20"><a href="#cite_note-20">[20]</a></sup> The god is known under this name in the <a href="/wiki/Rigvedic_deities" title="Rigvedic deities">Rigveda</a> (<a href="/wiki/Vedic_Sanskrit" title="Vedic Sanskrit">Vedic Sanskrit</a> <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dyaus_Pita" title="Dyaus Pita">Dyaus/Dyaus Pita</a></i>), <a href="/wiki/Latin" title="Latin">Latin</a> (compare <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Jupiter_(god)" title="Jupiter (god)">Jupiter</a></i>, from <i>Iuppiter</i>, deriving from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European" title="Proto-Indo-European">Proto-Indo-European</a> vocative *<i><span class="Unicode" lang="ine" style="white-space:nowrap;" title="Proto-Indo-European language">dyeu-ph<sub>2</sub>t&#275;r</span></i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-21"><a href="#cite_note-21">[21]</a></sup> deriving from the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/PIE_root" title="PIE root">root</a> *<i>dyeu</i>- ("to shine", and in its many derivatives, "sky, heaven, god").<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Zeus_19-1"><a href="#cite_note-Zeus-19">[19]</a></sup>\nZeus is the only deity in the Olympic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pantheon_(gods)" title="Pantheon (gods)">pantheon</a> whose name has such a transparent Indo-European etymology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[22]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The earliest attested forms of the name are the <a href="/wiki/Mycenaean_Greek" title="Mycenaean Greek">Mycenaean Greek</a> <span lang="gmy" title="Mycenaean Greek language text">&#65543;&#65592;</span>, <i>di-we</i> and <span lang="gmy" title="Mycenaean Greek language text">&#65543;&#65594;</span>, <i>di-wo</i>, written in the <a href="/wiki/Linear_B" title="Linear B">Linear B</a> syllabic script.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-23"><a href="#cite_note-23">[23]</a></sup>\n</p><p><a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>, in his <a href="/wiki/Cratylus_(dialogue)" title="Cratylus (dialogue)"><i>Cratylus</i></a>, gives a folk etymology of Zeus meaning "cause of life always to all things," because of puns between alternate titles of Zeus (<i>Zen</i> and <i>Dia</i>) with the Greek words for life and "because of."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[24]</a></sup>  This etymology, along with Plato\'s entire method of deriving etymologies, is not supported by modern scholarship.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[26]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Mythology">Mythology</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Birth">Birth</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Id%C3%A4ische_Grotte_01.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="3142" data-file-width="4188" decoding="async" height="165" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Id%C3%A4ische_Grotte_01.JPG/220px-Id%C3%A4ische_Grotte_01.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Id%C3%A4ische_Grotte_01.JPG/330px-Id%C3%A4ische_Grotte_01.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Id%C3%A4ische_Grotte_01.JPG/440px-Id%C3%A4ische_Grotte_01.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Id%C3%A4ische_Grotte_01.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>"<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cave_of_Zeus" title="Cave of Zeus">Cave of Zeus</a>", <a href="/wiki/Mount_Ida_(Crete)" title="Mount Ida (Crete)">Mount Ida, Crete</a></div></div></div>\n<p><a href="/wiki/Cronus" title="Cronus">Cronus</a> sired several children by <a href="/wiki/Rhea_(mythology)" title="Rhea (mythology)">Rhea</a>: <a href="/wiki/Hestia" title="Hestia">Hestia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Demeter" title="Demeter">Demeter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hera" title="Hera">Hera</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hades" title="Hades">Hades</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Poseidon" title="Poseidon">Poseidon</a>, but swallowed them all as soon as they were born, since he had learned from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gaia_(mythology)" title="Gaia (mythology)">Gaia</a> and <a href="/wiki/Uranus_(mythology)" title="Uranus (mythology)">Uranus</a> that he was destined to be overthrown by his son as he had previously overthrown Uranus, his own father, an oracle that Rhea heard and wished to avert.\n</p><p>When Zeus was about to be born, Rhea sought Gaia to devise a plan to save him, so that Cronus would get his retribution for his acts against Uranus and his own children.  Rhea gave birth to Zeus in Crete, handing Cronus a rock wrapped in swaddling clothes, which he promptly swallowed.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[27]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Infancy">Infancy</span></h3>\n<p>Varying versions of the story exist:\n</p>\n<ol><li>According to Hyginus (Fabulae, 139)) Zeus was raised by a nymph named Amalthea. Since Saturn (Cronus) ruled over the <a href="/wiki/Earth" title="Earth">Earth</a>, the <a href="/wiki/Heaven" title="Heaven">heavens</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Sea" title="Sea">sea</a>, she hid him by dangling him on a <a href="/wiki/Rope" title="Rope">rope</a> from a <a href="/wiki/Tree" title="Tree">tree</a> so he was suspended between earth, sea and sky and thus, invisible to his father.</li>\n<li>According to Pseudo-Apollodorus (Bibliotheca, <a class="external text" href="http://www.theoi.com/Text/Apollodorus1.html#1" rel="nofollow">1.1.5-7</a>)) Zeus was raised by a <a href="/wiki/Goat" title="Goat">goat</a> named <a href="/wiki/Amalthea_(mythology)" title="Amalthea (mythology)">Amalthea</a> in a cave called Dictaeon Antron (<a href="/wiki/Psychro_Cave" title="Psychro Cave">Psychro Cave</a>). A a company of soldiers called <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kouretes" title="Kouretes">Kouretes</a> danced, shouted and clashed their spears against their shields so that Cronus would not hear the baby\'s cry.</li></ol>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="King_of_the_gods">King of the gods</span></h3>\n<table class="box-Unreferenced_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Zeus&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">April 2018</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>After reaching manhood, Zeus forced Cronus to disgorge first the stone (which was set down at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pytho" title="Pytho">Pytho</a> under the glens of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Parnassus" title="Parnassus">Parnassus</a> to be a sign to mortal men, the <a href="/wiki/Omphalos" title="Omphalos">Omphalos</a>) then his siblings in reverse order of swallowing. In some versions, <a href="/wiki/Metis_(mythology)" title="Metis (mythology)">Metis</a> gave Cronus an <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Emetic" title="Emetic">emetic</a> to force him to disgorge the babies, or Zeus cut Cronus\'s <a href="/wiki/Stomach" title="Stomach">stomach</a> open. Then Zeus released the brothers of Cronus, the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Hekatonkheires" title="Hekatonkheires">Hecatonchires</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Cyclops" title="Cyclops">Cyclopes</a>, from their dungeon in <a href="/wiki/Tartarus" title="Tartarus">Tartarus</a>, killing their guard, <a href="/wiki/Campe" title="Campe">Campe</a>.\n</p><p>\nAs a token of their appreciation, the Cyclopes gave him <a href="/wiki/Thunder" title="Thunder">thunder</a> and the thunderbolt, or <a href="/wiki/Lightning" title="Lightning">lightning</a>, which had previously been hidden by Gaia. Together, Zeus, his brothers and sisters, Hecatonchires and Cyclopes overthrew Cronus and the other Titans, in the combat called the <a href="/wiki/Titanomachy" title="Titanomachy">Titanomachy</a>. The defeated Titans were then cast into a shadowy underworld region known as Tartarus. <a href="/wiki/Atlas_(mythology)" title="Atlas (mythology)">Atlas</a>, one of the titans who fought against Zeus, was punished by having to hold up the sky.</p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Zeus_Getty_Villa.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1000" data-file-width="602" decoding="async" height="365" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Zeus_Getty_Villa.jpg/220px-Zeus_Getty_Villa.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Zeus_Getty_Villa.jpg/330px-Zeus_Getty_Villa.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4d/Zeus_Getty_Villa.jpg/440px-Zeus_Getty_Villa.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Zeus_Getty_Villa.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>First century statue of Zeus</div></div></div>\n<p>After the battle with the Titans, Zeus shared the world with his elder brothers, <a href="/wiki/Poseidon" title="Poseidon">Poseidon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hades" title="Hades">Hades</a>, by drawing lots: Zeus got the sky and air, Poseidon the waters, and Hades the world of the dead (the underworld). The ancient Earth, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Gaia_(mythology)" title="Gaia (mythology)">Gaia</a>, could not be claimed; she was left to all three, each according to their capabilities, which explains why Poseidon was the "earth-shaker" (the god of earthquakes) and Hades claimed the humans who died (see also <a href="/wiki/Penthus" title="Penthus">Penthus</a>).\n</p><p>Gaia resented the way Zeus had treated the Titans, because they were her children. Soon after taking the throne as king of the gods, Zeus had to fight some of Gaia\'s other children, the <a href="/wiki/Monster" title="Monster">monsters</a> <a href="/wiki/Typhon" title="Typhon">Typhon</a> and <a href="/wiki/Echidna_(mythology)" title="Echidna (mythology)">Echidna</a>. He vanquished Typhon and trapped him under <a href="/wiki/Mount_Etna" title="Mount Etna">Mount Etna</a>, but left Echidna and her children alive.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Conflicts_with_humans">Conflicts with humans</span></h3><p>\nWhen Zeus was atop Mount Olympus he was appalled by human sacrifice and other signs of human decadence. He decided to wipe out mankind and flooded the world with the help of his brother <a href="/wiki/Poseidon" title="Poseidon">Poseidon</a>. After the flood, only <a href="/wiki/Deucalion" title="Deucalion">Deucalion</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Pyrrha" title="Pyrrha">Pyrrha</a> remained.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[28]</a></sup> This <a href="/wiki/Flood_myth" title="Flood myth">flood narrative</a> is a common motif in mythology.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[29]</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:The_Chariot_of_Zeus_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_14994.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="305" data-file-width="480" decoding="async" height="140" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/The_Chariot_of_Zeus_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_14994.png/220px-The_Chariot_of_Zeus_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_14994.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/The_Chariot_of_Zeus_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_14994.png/330px-The_Chariot_of_Zeus_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_14994.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/7c/The_Chariot_of_Zeus_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_14994.png/440px-The_Chariot_of_Zeus_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_14994.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:The_Chariot_of_Zeus_-_Project_Gutenberg_eText_14994.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Chariot of Zeus, from an 1879 <i>Stories from the Greek Tragedians</i> by Alfred Church.</div></div></div>\n<p>Throughout history Zeus has been depicted as using violence to get his way and terrorize humans. As god of the sky he has the power to hurl lightning bolts as a weapon. Since lightning is quite powerful and sometimes deadly, it is a bold sign when lightning strikes because it is known that Zeus most likely threw the bolt.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-30"><a href="#cite_note-30">[30]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="In_the_Iliad">In the <i>Iliad</i></span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:James_Barry_001.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2496" data-file-width="3149" decoding="async" height="238" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/James_Barry_001.jpg/300px-James_Barry_001.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/James_Barry_001.jpg/450px-James_Barry_001.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/d4/James_Barry_001.jpg/600px-James_Barry_001.jpg 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:James_Barry_001.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><i>Jupiter and Juno on Mount Ida</i> by <a href="/wiki/James_Barry_(painter)" title="James Barry (painter)">James Barry</a>, 1773 (City Art Galleries, Sheffield.)</div></div></div>\n<p>The <i><a href="/wiki/Iliad" title="Iliad">Iliad</a></i> is a poem by <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a> about the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Trojan_war" title="Trojan war">Trojan war</a> and the battle over the City of <a href="/wiki/Troy" title="Troy">Troy</a>. As God of the sky, lightning, thunder, law, order, justice, Zeus controlled <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">ancient Greece</a> and all of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mortals" title="Mortals">mortals</a> and <a href="/wiki/Immortality" title="Immortality">immortals</a> living there.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[31]</a></sup> The Iliad covers the Trojan War, in which Zeus plays a major part.\n</p><p>Notable Scenes that include Zeus<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-32"><a href="#cite_note-32">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-33"><a href="#cite_note-33">[33]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<ul><li>Book 2: Zeus sends <a href="/wiki/Agamemnon" title="Agamemnon">Agamemnon</a> a dream and is able to partially control his decisions because of the effects of the dream</li>\n<li>Book 4: Zeus promises <a href="/wiki/Hera" title="Hera">Hera</a> to ultimately destroy the City of Troy at the end of the war</li>\n<li>Book 7: Zeus and <a href="/wiki/Poseidon" title="Poseidon">Poseidon</a> ruin the <a href="/wiki/Achaeans_(Homer)" title="Achaeans (Homer)">Achaeans</a> fortress</li>\n<li>Book 8: Zeus prohibits the other Gods from fighting each other and has to return to <a href="/wiki/Mount_Ida" title="Mount Ida">Mount Ida</a> where he can think over his decision that the Greeks will lose the war</li>\n<li>Book 14: Zeus is seduced by <a href="/wiki/Hera" title="Hera">Hera</a> and becomes distracted while she helps out the Greeks</li>\n<li>Book 15: Zeus wakes up and realizes that <a href="/wiki/Poseidon" title="Poseidon">Poseidon</a> his own brother has been helping out the Greeks, while also sending <a href="/wiki/Hector" title="Hector">Hector</a> and <a href="/wiki/Apollo" title="Apollo">Apollo</a> to help fight the Trojans ensuring that the City of Troy will fall</li>\n<li>Book 16: Zeus is upset that he couldn\'t help save Sarpedon\'s life because it would then contradict his previous decisions</li>\n<li>Book 17: Zeus is emotionally hurt by the fate of <a href="/wiki/Hector" title="Hector">Hector</a></li>\n<li>Book 20: Zeus lets the other Gods help out their respective sides in the war</li>\n<li>Book 24: Zeus demands that <a href="/wiki/Achilles" title="Achilles">Achilles</a> release the corpse of <a href="/wiki/Hector" title="Hector">Hector</a> to be buried honourably</li></ul>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="List_of_other_deeds">List of other deeds</span></h3>\n<ul><li>Zeus granted <a href="/wiki/Callirrhoe_(daughter_of_Achelous)" title="Callirrhoe (daughter of Achelous)">Callirrhoe\'s</a> prayer that her sons by <a href="/wiki/Alcmaeon_(mythology)" title="Alcmaeon (mythology)">Alcmaeon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Acarnan" title="Acarnan">Acarnan</a> and <a href="/wiki/Amphoterus_(son_of_Alcmaeon)" title="Amphoterus (son of Alcmaeon)">Amphoterus</a>, grow quickly so that they might be able to avenger the death of their father by the hands of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Phegeus_(king_of_Psophis)" title="Phegeus (king of Psophis)">Phegeus</a> and his two sons.</li>\n<li>He unsuccessfully wooed Thetis, daughter of Nereus.</li>\n<li class="mw-empty-elt"></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Family">Family</span></h2>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Zeus_and_Hera">Zeus and Hera</span></h3>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Hera" title="Hera">Hera</a></div>\n<p>Zeus was brother and consort of <a href="/wiki/Hera" title="Hera">Hera</a>. By Hera, Zeus sired <a href="/wiki/Ares" title="Ares">Ares</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hebe_(mythology)" title="Hebe (mythology)">Hebe</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hephaestus" title="Hephaestus">Hephaestus</a>, though some accounts say that Hera produced these offspring alone. Some also include <a href="/wiki/Eileithyia" title="Eileithyia">Eileithyia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eris_(mythology)" title="Eris (mythology)">Eris</a>, <a href="/wiki/Enyo" title="Enyo">Enyo</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Angelos_(Greek_mythology)" title="Angelos (Greek mythology)">Angelos</a> as their daughters. In the section of the Iliad known to scholars as the <a href="/wiki/Deception_of_Zeus" title="Deception of Zeus">Deception of Zeus</a>, the two of them are described as having begun their sexual relationship without their parents knowing about it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-34"><a href="#cite_note-34">[34]</a></sup> The conquests of Zeus among <a href="/wiki/Nymph" title="Nymph">nymphs</a> and the mythic mortal progenitors of <a href="/wiki/Greeks" title="Greeks">Hellenic</a> dynasties are famous. Olympian mythography even credits him with unions with <a href="/wiki/Leto" title="Leto">Leto</a>, <a href="/wiki/Demeter" title="Demeter">Demeter</a>, <a href="/wiki/Metis_(mythology)" title="Metis (mythology)">Metis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Themis" title="Themis">Themis</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eurynome_(Oceanid)" title="Eurynome (Oceanid)">Eurynome</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mnemosyne" title="Mnemosyne">Mnemosyne</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-35"><a href="#cite_note-35">[35]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-36"><a href="#cite_note-36">[36]</a></sup> Other relationships with immortals included <a href="/wiki/Dione_(Titaness)" title="Dione (Titaness)">Dione</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Maia_(mythology)" title="Maia (mythology)">Maia</a>. Among mortals were <a href="/wiki/Semele" title="Semele">Semele</a>, <a href="/wiki/Io_(mythology)" title="Io (mythology)">Io</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Europa_(mythology)" title="Europa (mythology)">Europa</a> and <a href="/wiki/Leda_(mythology)" title="Leda (mythology)">Leda</a> (for more details, see below) and with the young <a href="/wiki/Ganymede_(mythology)" title="Ganymede (mythology)">Ganymede</a> (although he was mortal Zeus granted him eternal youth and immortality).\n</p><p>Many myths render Hera as jealous of his amorous conquests and a consistent enemy of Zeus\'s mistresses and their children by him. For a time, a <a href="/wiki/Nymph" title="Nymph">nymph</a> named <a href="/wiki/Echo_(mythology)" title="Echo (mythology)">Echo</a> had the job of distracting Hera from his affairs by talking incessantly, and when Hera discovered the deception, she cursed Echo to repeat the words of others.\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Transformation_of_Zeus">Transformation of Zeus</span></h3>\n<table class="wikitable">\n<tbody><tr>\n<th>Love interest\n</th>\n<th>Disguises\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><a href="/wiki/Aegina_(mythology)" title="Aegina
