title="Veritas">Aletheia</a>\n</td>\n<th>\n</th>\n<td>\n</td>\n<td><i>No mother</i>\n</td>\n<td>&#8226; <a href="/wiki/Orion_(mythology)" title="Orion (mythology)">Orion</a>\n</td></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i>Unknown mother</i>\n</td>\n<td>&#8226; <a href="/wiki/At%C3%AB" title="At&#235;">Ate</a>\n</td>\n<td>\n</td>\n<th>\n</th>\n<td>\n</td>\n<th>\n</th></tr>\n<tr>\n<td><i>Unknown mother</i>\n</td>\n<td>&#8226; <a href="/wiki/Caerus" title="Caerus">Caerus</a>\n</td>\n<th>\n</th>\n<td>\n</td>\n<th>\n</th>\n<td>\n</td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p><sup>1</sup>The Greeks variously claimed that the Moires/Fates were the daughters of Zeus and the Titaness <a href="/wiki/Themis" title="Themis">Themis</a> or of primordial beings like <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Chaos_(mythology)" title="Chaos (mythology)">Chaos</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Nyx_(mythology)" title="Nyx (mythology)">Nyx</a>, or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ananke_(mythology)" title="Ananke (mythology)">Ananke</a>.\n</p><p><sup>2</sup>The Charites/Graces were usually considered the daughters of Zeus and Eurynome but they were also said to be daughters of Dionysus and Aphrodite or of Helios and the naiad Aegle.\n</p><p><sup>3</sup>Some accounts say that Ares, Hebe, and Hephaestus were born parthenogenetically.\n</p><p><sup>4</sup>According to one version, Athena is said to be born parthenogenetically.\n</p><p><sup>5</sup>Helen was either the daughter of Leda or Nemesis.\n</p><p><sup>6</sup>Tyche is usually considered a daughter of Aphrodite and Hermes.\n</p><p><span id="Zeus_Kasios"></span><span id="Zeus_Adados"></span><span id="Heliopolite_Zeus"></span><span id="Heliopolitan_Zeus"></span><span id="Zeus_Helioupolites"></span>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Roles_and_epithets">Roles and epithets</span></h2>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">See also: <a href="/wiki/Category:Epithets_of_Zeus" title="Category:Epithets of Zeus">Category:Epithets of Zeus</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Bust_of_Zeus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="640" data-file-width="548" decoding="async" height="257" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Bust_of_Zeus.jpg/220px-Bust_of_Zeus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Bust_of_Zeus.jpg/330px-Bust_of_Zeus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/01/Bust_of_Zeus.jpg/440px-Bust_of_Zeus.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Bust_of_Zeus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Roman marble colossal head of Zeus, 2nd century AD (<a href="/wiki/British_Museum" title="British Museum">British Museum</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-71"><a href="#cite_note-71">[71]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Zeus played a dominant role, presiding over the <a href="/wiki/Ancient_Greece" title="Ancient Greece">Greek</a> Olympian pantheon. He fathered many of the heroes  and was featured in many of their <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cult_(religion)" title="Cult (religion)">local cults</a>. Though the Homeric "cloud collector" was the god of the sky and thunder like his Near-Eastern counterparts, he was also the supreme cultural artifact; in some senses, he was the embodiment of Greek <a href="/wiki/Religion" title="Religion">religious</a> beliefs and the <a href="/wiki/Archetype" title="Archetype">archetypal</a> Greek deity.\n</p><p>Aside from local epithets that simply designated the deity as doing something random at some particular place, the <a href="/wiki/Epithet" title="Epithet">epithets</a> or titles applied to Zeus emphasized different aspects of his wide-ranging authority:\n</p>\n<ul><li><b>Zeus Aegiduchos</b> or <b>Aegiochos</b>: Usually taken as Zeus as the bearer of the <a href="/wiki/Aegis" title="Aegis">Aegis</a>, the divine shield with the head of <a href="/wiki/Medusa" title="Medusa">Medusa</a> across it,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-72"><a href="#cite_note-72">[72]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[73]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-74"><a href="#cite_note-74">[74]</a></sup> although others derive it from "goat" (<span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">&#945;&#7988;&#958;</span>) and <i>okh&#275;</i> (<span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">&#959;&#967;&#942;</span>) in reference to Zeus\'s nurse, the divine goat <a href="/wiki/Amalthea_(mythology)" title="Amalthea (mythology)">Amalthea</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-75"><a href="#cite_note-75">[75]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-76"><a href="#cite_note-76">[76]</a></sup></li>\n<li><b>Zeus <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Agoraeus" title="Agoraeus">Agoraeus</a></b>: Zeus as patron of the marketplace (<a href="/wiki/Agora" title="Agora">agora</a>) and punisher of dishonest traders.</li>\n<li><b><a href="/wiki/Zeus_Areius" title="Zeus Areius">Zeus Areius</a></b>: either "warlike" or "the atoning one".</li>\n<li><b>Zeus Horkios</b>: Zeus as keeper of oaths. Exposed liars were made to dedicate a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Votive" title="Votive">votive</a> statue to Zeus, often at the sanctuary at Olympia</li>\n<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zeus_Olympios" title="Zeus Olympios">Zeus Olympios</a></b>: Zeus as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/King_of_the_gods" title="King of the gods">king of the gods</a> and patron of the <a href="/wiki/Panhellenic_Games" title="Panhellenic Games">Panhellenic Games</a> at <a href="/wiki/Olympia,_Greece" title="Olympia, Greece">Olympia</a></li>\n<li><b>Zeus Panhellenios</b> ("Zeus of All the <a href="/wiki/Names_of_the_Greeks" title="Names of the Greeks">Greeks</a>"): worshipped at <a href="/wiki/Aeacus" title="Aeacus">Aeacus</a>\'s temple on <a href="/wiki/Aegina" title="Aegina">Aegina</a></li>\n<li><b>Zeus Xenios</b>, <b>Philoxenon</b>, or <b>Hospites</b>: Zeus as the patron of hospitality (<i><a href="/wiki/Xenia_(Greek)" title="Xenia (Greek)">xenia</a></i>) and guests, avenger of wrongs done to strangers</li></ul>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Otricoli_Zeus_-_1889_drawing.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1448" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="319" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Otricoli_Zeus_-_1889_drawing.jpg/220px-Otricoli_Zeus_-_1889_drawing.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Otricoli_Zeus_-_1889_drawing.jpg/330px-Otricoli_Zeus_-_1889_drawing.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b2/Otricoli_Zeus_-_1889_drawing.jpg/440px-Otricoli_Zeus_-_1889_drawing.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Otricoli_Zeus_-_1889_drawing.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A bust of Zeus.</div></div></div>\n<p>Additional names and epithets for Zeus are also:\n</p>\n<ul><li><b>Abrettenus</b> (&#7944;&#946;&#961;&#949;&#964;&#964;&#951;&#957;&#972;&#962;) or <b>Abretanus</b>: surname of Zeus in <a href="/wiki/Mysia" title="Mysia">Mysia</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-77"><a href="#cite_note-77">[77]</a></sup></li>\n<li><b>Achad</b>: one of his names in <a href="/wiki/Syria" title="Syria">Syria</a>.</li>\n<li><b>Acraeus</b>: his name at <a href="/wiki/Smyrna" title="Smyrna">Smyrna</a>.</li>\n<li><b>Acrettenus</b>: his name in Mysia.</li>\n<li><b>Adad</b>: one of his names in Syria.</li>\n<li><b>Adultus</b>: from his being invoked by <i>adults</i>, on their marriage.</li>\n<li><b><a href="/wiki/Apemius" title="Apemius">Apemius</a></b>: Zeus as the averter of ills</li>\n<li><b><a href="/wiki/Apomyius" title="Apomyius">Apomyius</a></b> Zeus as one who dispels flies</li>\n<li><b>Astrapios</b> ("Lightninger"): Zeus as a <a href="/wiki/Weather_god" title="Weather god">weather god</a></li>\n<li><b>Bottiaeus</b>: Worshipped at <a href="/wiki/Antioch" title="Antioch">Antioch</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-78"><a href="#cite_note-78">[78]</a></sup></li>\n<li><b>Brontios</b> ("Thunderer"): Zeus as a <a href="/wiki/Weather_god" title="Weather god">weather god</a></li>\n<li><b>Diktaios</b>: Zeus as lord of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dikte" title="Dikte">Dikte</a> mountain range, worshipped from <a href="/wiki/Mycenaean_Greece" title="Mycenaean Greece">Mycenaean times</a> on Crete<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-79"><a href="#cite_note-79">[79]</a></sup></li>\n<li><b>Ithomatas</b>: Worshipped at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mount_Ithome" title="Mount Ithome">Mount Ithome</a> in Messenia</li>\n<li><b>Zeus Adados</b>: A Hellenization of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canaanite_religion" title="Canaanite religion">Canaanite</a> <a href="/wiki/Hadad" title="Hadad">Hadad</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Assyrian_religion" title="Assyrian religion">Assyrian</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Adad" title="Adad">Adad</a>, particularly his solar cult at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heliopolis_(Syria)" title="Heliopolis (Syria)">Heliopolis</a><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cook_80-0"><a href="#cite_note-cook-80">[80]</a></sup></li>\n<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zeus_Bouleus" title="Zeus Bouleus">Zeus Bouleus</a></b>: Worshipped at <a href="/wiki/Dodona" title="Dodona">Dodona</a>, the earliest <a href="/wiki/Oracle" title="Oracle">oracle</a>, along with Zeus Naos</li>\n<li><b>Zeus Georgos</b> (<span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">&#918;&#949;&#8058;&#962; &#915;&#949;&#969;&#961;&#947;&#972;&#962;</span>, "Zeus the Farmer"): Zeus as god of crops and the harvest, worshipped in <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Athens" title="Ancient Athens">Athens</a></li>\n<li><b>Zeus Helioupolites</b> ("Heliopolite" or "Heliopolitan Zeus"): A Hellenization of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canaanite_religion" title="Canaanite religion">Canaanite</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ba%CA%BFal" title="Ba&#703;al">Ba&#703;al</a> (probably <a href="/wiki/Hadad" title="Hadad">Hadad</a>) worshipped as a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sun_god" title="Sun god">sun god</a> at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heliopolis_(Syria)" title="Heliopolis (Syria)">Heliopolis</a> (modern <a href="/wiki/Baalbek" title="Baalbek">Baalbek</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-cook_80-1"><a href="#cite_note-cook-80">[80]</a></sup></li>\n<li><b>Zeus Kasios</b> ("Zeus of Mount Kasios" the modern <a href="/wiki/Jebel_Aqra" title="Jebel Aqra">Jebel Aqra</a>): Worshipped at a site on the Syrian&#8211;Turkish border, a Hellenization of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Canaanite_religion" title="Canaanite religion">Canaanite</a> mountain and <a href="/wiki/Weather_god" title="Weather god">weather god</a> <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baal_Zephon" title="Baal Zephon">Baal Zephon</a></li>\n<li><b>Zeus Labrandos</b> ("Zeus of <a href="/wiki/Labraunda" title="Labraunda">Labraunda</a>"): Worshiped at <a href="/wiki/Caria" title="Caria">Caria</a>, depicted with a double-edged axe (<i><a href="/wiki/Labrys" title="Labrys">labrys</a></i>), a Hellenization of the <a href="/wiki/Hurrian_religion" title="Hurrian religion">Hurrian</a> <a href="/wiki/Weather_god" title="Weather god">weather god</a> <a href="/wiki/Teshub" title="Teshub">Teshub</a></li>\n<li><b>Zeus Meilichios</b> ("Zeus the Easily-Entreated"): Worshipped at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ancient_Athens" title="Ancient Athens">Athens</a>, a form of the archaic chthonic <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Daimon" title="Daimon">daimon</a></i> <a href="/wiki/Meilichios" title="Meilichios">Meilichios</a></li>\n<li><b><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Zeus_Naos" title="Zeus Naos">Zeus Naos</a></b>: Worshipped at <a href="/wiki/Dodona" title="Dodona">Dodona</a>, the earliest <a href="/wiki/Oracle" title="Oracle">oracle</a>, along with Zeus Bouleus</li>\n<li><b><a href="/wiki/Talos" title="Talos">Zeus Tallaios</a></b> ("Solar Zeus"): Worshipped on <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a></li></ul>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Cults_of_Zeus">Cults of Zeus</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Marble_eagle_with_open_wings,_from_the_sanctuary_of_Zeus_Hypsistos,_Archaeological_Museum,_Dion_(7080054119).jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="4288" data-file-width="3216" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Marble_eagle_with_open_wings%2C_from_the_sanctuary_of_Zeus_Hypsistos%2C_Archaeological_Museum%2C_Dion_%287080054119%29.jpg/170px-Marble_eagle_with_open_wings%2C_from_the_sanctuary_of_Zeus_Hypsistos%2C_Archaeological_Museum%2C_Dion_%287080054119%29.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Marble_eagle_with_open_wings%2C_from_the_sanctuary_of_Zeus_Hypsistos%2C_Archaeological_Museum%2C_Dion_%287080054119%29.jpg/255px-Marble_eagle_with_open_wings%2C_from_the_sanctuary_of_Zeus_Hypsistos%2C_Archaeological_Museum%2C_Dion_%287080054119%29.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1b/Marble_eagle_with_open_wings%2C_from_the_sanctuary_of_Zeus_Hypsistos%2C_Archaeological_Museum%2C_Dion_%287080054119%29.jpg/340px-Marble_eagle_with_open_wings%2C_from_the_sanctuary_of_Zeus_Hypsistos%2C_Archaeological_Museum%2C_Dion_%287080054119%29.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Marble_eagle_with_open_wings,_from_the_sanctuary_of_Zeus_Hypsistos,_Archaeological_Museum,_Dion_(7080054119).jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Marble eagle from the sanctuary of <i>Zeus Hypsistos</i>, <a href="/wiki/Archaeological_Museum_of_Dion" title="Archaeological Museum of Dion">Archaeological Museum of Dion</a>.</div></div></div>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Panhellenic_cults">Panhellenic cults</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:172px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Statue_of_Zeus_dsc02611-.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2816" data-file-width="2112" decoding="async" height="227" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Statue_of_Zeus_dsc02611-.jpg/170px-Statue_of_Zeus_dsc02611-.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Statue_of_Zeus_dsc02611-.jpg/255px-Statue_of_Zeus_dsc02611-.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/13/Statue_of_Zeus_dsc02611-.jpg/340px-Statue_of_Zeus_dsc02611-.jpg 2x" width="170"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Statue_of_Zeus_dsc02611-.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Colossal seated <a href="/wiki/Dagon" title="Dagon">Marnas</a> from <a href="/wiki/Gaza_City" title="Gaza City">Gaza</a> portrayed in the style of Zeus.  Roman period Marnas<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[81]</a></sup> was the chief divinity of Gaza (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Istanbul_Archaeology_Museum" title="Istanbul Archaeology Museum">Istanbul Archaeology Museum</a>).</div></div></div><p>The major center where all Greeks converged to pay honor to their chief god was <a href="/wiki/Olympia,_Greece" title="Olympia, Greece">Olympia</a>. Their quadrennial <a href="/wiki/Festival" title="Festival">festival</a> featured the famous Games. There was also an altar to Zeus made not of stone, but of ash, from the accumulated remains of many centuries\' worth of animals sacrificed there.\n</p><p>Outside of the major inter-<a href="/wiki/Polis" title="Polis">polis</a> sanctuaries, there were no modes of worshipping Zeus precisely shared across the Greek world. Most of the titles listed below, for instance, could be found at any number of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Greek_temple" title="Greek temple">Greek temples</a> from <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Asia_Minor" title="Asia Minor">Asia Minor</a> to <a href="/wiki/Sicily" title="Sicily">Sicily</a>. Certain modes of ritual were held in common as well: sacrificing a white animal over a raised altar, for instance.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Zeus_Velchanos">Zeus Velchanos</span></h4>\n<p>With one exception, Greeks were unanimous in recognizing the birthplace of Zeus as Crete. Minoan culture contributed many essentials of ancient Greek religion: "by a hundred channels the old civilization emptied itself into the new", Will Durant observed,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-82"><a href="#cite_note-82">[82]</a></sup> and Cretan Zeus retained his youthful Minoan features. The local child of the Great Mother, "a small and inferior deity who took the roles of son and consort",<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-83"><a href="#cite_note-83">[83]</a></sup> whose Minoan name the Greeks Hellenized as Velchanos, was in time assumed as an <a href="/wiki/Epithet" title="Epithet">epithet</a> by Zeus, as transpired at many other sites, and he came to be venerated in Crete as <b>Zeus Velchanos</b> ("boy-Zeus"), often simply the <i><a href="/wiki/Kouros" title="Kouros">Kouros</a></i>.\n</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a>, Zeus was worshipped at a number of caves at <a href="/wiki/Knossos" title="Knossos">Knossos</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Mount_Ida,_Crete" title="Mount Ida, Crete">Ida</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Palaikastro" title="Palaikastro">Palaikastro</a>. In the Hellenistic period a small sanctuary dedicated to Zeus Velchanos was founded at the <a href="/wiki/Hagia_Triada" title="Hagia Triada">Hagia Triada</a> site of a long-ruined Minoan palace. Broadly contemporary coins from <a href="/wiki/Phaistos" title="Phaistos">Phaistos</a> show the form under which he was worshiped: a youth sits among the branches of a tree, with a cockerel on his knees.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-84"><a href="#cite_note-84">[84]</a></sup> On other Cretan coins Velchanos is represented as an eagle and in association with a goddess celebrating a mystic marriage.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-85"><a href="#cite_note-85">[85]</a></sup> Inscriptions at <a href="/wiki/Gortyn" title="Gortyn">Gortyn</a> and Lyttos record a <i>Velchania</i> festival, showing that Velchanios was still widely venerated in Hellenistic Crete.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-86"><a href="#cite_note-86">[86]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The stories of <a href="/wiki/Minos" title="Minos">Minos</a> and <a href="/wiki/Epimenides" title="Epimenides">Epimenides</a> suggest that these caves were once used for <a href="/wiki/Incubation_(ritual)" title="Incubation (ritual)">incubatory</a> divination by kings and priests. The dramatic setting of <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>\'s <i>Laws</i> is along the pilgrimage-route to one such site, emphasizing archaic Cretan knowledge. On Crete, Zeus was represented in art as a long-haired youth rather than a mature adult and hymned as <i>ho megas kouros</i>, "the great youth". Ivory statuettes of the "Divine Boy" were unearthed near the <a href="/wiki/Labyrinth" title="Labyrinth">Labyrinth</a> at <a href="/wiki/Knossos" title="Knossos">Knossos</a> by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sir_Arthur_Evans" title="Sir Arthur Evans">Sir Arthur Evans</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-87"><a href="#cite_note-87">[87]</a></sup> With the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kouretes" title="Kouretes">Kouretes</a>, a band of ecstatic armed dancers, he presided over the rigorous military-athletic training and secret rites of the Cretan <i><a href="/wiki/Paideia" title="Paideia">paideia</a></i>.\n</p><p>The myth of the death of Cretan Zeus, localised in numerous mountain sites though only mentioned in a comparatively late source, <a href="/wiki/Callimachus" title="Callimachus">Callimachus</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[88]</a></sup> together with the assertion of <a href="/wiki/Antoninus_Liberalis" title="Antoninus Liberalis">Antoninus Liberalis</a> that a fire shone forth annually from the birth-cave the infant shared with a <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bees_(mythology)" title="Bees (mythology)">mythic swarm of bees</a>, suggests that Velchanos had been an annual vegetative spirit.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[89]</a></sup>\nThe Hellenistic writer <a href="/wiki/Euhemerus" title="Euhemerus">Euhemerus</a> apparently proposed a theory that Zeus had actually been a great king of <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Crete</a> and that posthumously, his glory had slowly turned him into a deity. The works of Euhemerus himself have not survived, but Christian patristic writers took up the suggestion.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Zeus_Lykaios">Zeus Lykaios</span></h4>\n<div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Lykaia" title="Lykaia">Lykaia</a></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Stater_Zeus_Lampsacus_CdM.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2200" data-file-width="2200" decoding="async" height="220" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Stater_Zeus_Lampsacus_CdM.jpg/220px-Stater_Zeus_Lampsacus_CdM.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Stater_Zeus_Lampsacus_CdM.jpg/330px-Stater_Zeus_Lampsacus_CdM.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/56/Stater_Zeus_Lampsacus_CdM.jpg/440px-Stater_Zeus_Lampsacus_CdM.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Stater_Zeus_Lampsacus_CdM.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Laurel_wreath" title="Laurel wreath">Laurel-wreathed</a> head of Zeus on a gold <a href="/wiki/Stater" title="Stater">stater</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lampsacus" title="Lampsacus">Lampsacus</a>, c 360&#8211;340 BC (<a href="/wiki/Cabinet_des_M%C3%A9dailles" title="Cabinet des M&#233;dailles">Cabinet des M&#233;dailles</a>).</div></div></div>\n<p>The epithet <b>Zeus Lykaios</b> ("wolf-Zeus") is assumed by Zeus only in connection with the archaic festival of the <a href="/wiki/Lykaia" title="Lykaia">Lykaia</a> on the slopes of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lycaeus" title="Lycaeus">Mount Lykaion</a> ("Wolf Mountain"), the tallest peak in rustic <a href="/wiki/Arcadia" title="Arcadia">Arcadia</a>; Zeus had only a formal connection<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-90"><a href="#cite_note-90">[90]</a></sup> with the rituals and myths of this primitive <a href="/wiki/Rite_of_passage" title="Rite of passage">rite of passage</a> with an ancient threat of <a href="/wiki/Cannibalism" title="Cannibalism">cannibalism</a> and the possibility of a <a href="/wiki/Werewolf" title="Werewolf">werewolf</a> transformation for the <a href="/wiki/Ephebos" title="Ephebos">ephebes</a> who were the participants.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-91"><a href="#cite_note-91">[91]</a></sup> Near the ancient ash-heap where the sacrifices took place<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[92]</a></sup> was a forbidden precinct in which, allegedly, no shadows were ever cast.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[93]</a></sup>\n</p><p>According to <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-94"><a href="#cite_note-94">[94]</a></sup>  a particular clan would gather on the mountain to make a sacrifice every nine years to Zeus Lykaios, and a single morsel of human entrails would be intermingled with the animal\'s. Whoever ate the human flesh was said to turn into a wolf, and could only regain human form if he did not eat again of human flesh until the next nine-year cycle had ended. There were games associated with the Lykaia, removed in the fourth century to the first urbanization of Arcadia, <a href="/wiki/Megalopolis,_Greece" title="Megalopolis, Greece">Megalopolis</a>; there the major temple was dedicated to Zeus Lykaios.\n</p><p>\nThere is, however, the crucial detail that <i>Lykaios</i> or <i>Lykeios</i> (epithets of Zeus and Apollo) may derive from <a href="/wiki/Proto-Greek_language" title="Proto-Greek language">Proto-Greek</a> *<span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">&#955;&#973;&#954;&#951;</span>, "light", a noun still attested in compounds such as <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">&#7936;&#956;&#966;&#953;&#955;&#973;&#954;&#951;</span>, "twilight", <span lang="grc" title="Ancient Greek language text">&#955;&#965;&#954;&#940;&#946;&#945;&#962;</span>, "year" (lit. "light\'s course") etc. This, Cook argues, brings indeed much new \'light\' to the matter as <a href="/wiki/Achaeus_of_Eretria" title="Achaeus of Eretria">Achaeus</a>, the contemporary tragedian of <a href="/wiki/Sophocles" title="Sophocles">Sophocles</a>, spoke of Zeus Lykaios as "starry-eyed", and this Zeus Lykaios may just be the Arcadian Zeus, son of Aether, described by <a href="/wiki/Cicero" title="Cicero">Cicero</a>. Again under this new signification may be seen <a href="/wiki/Pausanias_(geographer)" title="Pausanias (geographer)">Pausanias</a>\' descriptions of Lykosoura being \'the first city that ever the sun beheld\', and of the altar of Zeus, at the summit of Mount Lykaion, before which stood two columns bearing gilded eagles and \'facing the sun-rise\'. Further Cook sees only the tale of Zeus\' sacred precinct at Mount Lykaion allowing no shadows referring to Zeus as \'god of light\' (Lykaios).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[95]</a></sup></p><div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Statue_of_Zeus.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="570" data-file-width="700" decoding="async" height="179" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Statue_of_Zeus.jpg/220px-Statue_of_Zeus.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Statue_of_Zeus.jpg/330px-Statue_of_Zeus.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/c6/Statue_of_Zeus.jpg/440px-Statue_of_Zeus.jpg 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Statue_of_Zeus.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A statue of Zeus in a drawing.</div></div></div>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Additional_cults_of_Zeus">Additional cults of Zeus</span></h4>\n<table class="box-Unreferenced_section plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content ambox-Unreferenced" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Question_book-new.svg"><img alt="" data-file-height="399" data-file-width="512" decoding="async" height="39" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/50px-Question_book-new.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/75px-Question_book-new.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/9/99/Question_book-new.svg/100px-Question_book-new.svg.png 2x" width="50"/></a></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This section <b>does not <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">cite</a> any <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">sources</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please help <a class="external text" href="//en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Zeus&amp;action=edit">improve this section</a> by <a href="/wiki/Help:Introduction_to_referencing_with_Wiki_Markup/1" title="Help:Introduction to referencing with Wiki Markup/1">adding citations to reliable sources</a>. Unsourced material may be challenged and <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Verifiability#Burden_of_evidence" title="Wikipedia:Verifiability">removed</a>.</span> <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">August 2017</span>)</i></small><small class="hide-when-compact"><i> (<a href="/wiki/Help:Maintenance_template_removal" title="Help:Maintenance template removal">Learn how and when to remove this template message</a>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Although etymology indicates that Zeus was originally a sky god, many Greek cities honored a local Zeus who lived underground. Athenians and Sicilians honored <b>Zeus Meilichios</b> ("kindly" or "honeyed") while other cities had <b>Zeus Chthonios</b> ("earthy"), <b>Zeus Katachthonios</b> ("under-the-earth") and <b>Zeus Plousios</b> ("wealth-bringing"). These deities might be represented as snakes or in human form in visual art, or, for emphasis as both together in one image. They also received offerings of black animal victims sacrificed into sunken pits, as did <a href="/wiki/Chthonic" title="Chthonic">chthonic</a> deities like <a href="/wiki/Persephone" title="Persephone">Persephone</a> and <a href="/wiki/Demeter" title="Demeter">Demeter</a>, and also the <a href="/wiki/Hero" title="Hero">heroes</a> at their tombs. Olympian gods, by contrast, usually received white victims sacrificed upon raised altars.\n</p><p>In some cases, cities were not entirely sure whether the <i>daimon</i> to whom they sacrificed was a hero or an underground Zeus. Thus the shrine at Lebadaea in <a href="/wiki/Boeotia" title="Boeotia">Boeotia</a> might belong to the hero <a href="/wiki/Trophonius" title="Trophonius">Trophonius</a> or to <b>Zeus Trephonius</b> ("the nurturing"), depending on whether you believe <a href="/wiki/Pausanias_(geographer)" title="Pausanias (geographer)">Pausanias</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a>. The hero <a href="/wiki/Amphiaraus" title="Amphiaraus">Amphiaraus</a> was honored as <b>Zeus Amphiaraus</b> at Oropus outside of <a href="/wiki/Thebes,_Greece" title="Thebes, Greece">Thebes</a>, and the Spartans even had a shrine to <b>Zeus Agamemnon</b>. Ancient <a href="/wiki/Molossians" title="Molossians">Molossian</a> kings sacrificed to <b><a href="/wiki/Zeus_Areius" title="Zeus Areius">Zeus Areius</a></b>. <a href="/wiki/Strabo" title="Strabo">Strabo</a> mention that at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Tralles" title="Tralles">Tralles</a> there was the <b>Zeus Larisaeus</b>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[96]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Non-panhellenic_cults">Non-panhellenic cults</span></h3>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Museo_Barracco_-_Giove_Ammone_1010637.JPG"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2816" data-file-width="2112" decoding="async" height="293" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Museo_Barracco_-_Giove_Ammone_1010637.JPG/220px-Museo_Barracco_-_Giove_Ammone_1010637.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Museo_Barracco_-_Giove_Ammone_1010637.JPG/330px-Museo_Barracco_-_Giove_Ammone_1010637.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f0/Museo_Barracco_-_Giove_Ammone_1010637.JPG/440px-Museo_Barracco_-_Giove_Ammone_1010637.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Museo_Barracco_-_Giove_Ammone_1010637.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Roman cast <a href="/wiki/Terracotta" title="Terracotta">terracotta</a> of ram-horned <i>Jupiter Ammon</i>, 1st century AD (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Museo_Barracco" title="Museo Barracco">Museo Barracco</a>, Rome).</div></div></div>\n<p>In addition to the Panhellenic titles and conceptions listed above, local cults maintained their own idiosyncratic ideas about the king of gods and men.  With the epithet <b>Zeus <a href="/wiki/Aetnaeus" title="Aetnaeus">Aetnaeus</a></b> he was worshiped on <a href="/wiki/Mount_Etna" title="Mount Etna">Mount Aetna</a>, where there was a statue of him, and a local festival called the Aetnaea in his honor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[97]</a></sup> Other examples are listed below. As <b>Zeus Aeneius</b> or <b>Zeus Aenesius</b>, he was worshiped in the island of <a href="/wiki/Cephalonia" title="Cephalonia">Cephalonia</a>, where he had a temple on <a href="/wiki/Mount_Ainos" title="Mount Ainos">Mount Aenos</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[98]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Oracles_of_Zeus">Oracles of Zeus</span></h3>\n<p>Although most oracle sites were usually dedicated to <a href="/wiki/Apollo" title="Apollo">Apollo</a>, the heroes, or various goddesses like <a href="/wiki/Themis" title="Themis">Themis</a>, a few oracular sites were dedicated to Zeus. In addition, some foreign oracles, such as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ba%CA%BFal" title="Ba&#703;al">Ba&#703;al</a>\'s at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Heliopolis_(Syria)" title="Heliopolis (Syria)">Heliopolis</a>, were <a href="/wiki/Interpretatio_graeca" title="Interpretatio graeca">associated</a> with Zeus in Greek or <a href="/wiki/Jupiter" title="Jupiter">Jupiter</a> in Latin.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Oracle_at_Dodona">The Oracle at Dodona</span></h4>\n<p>The cult of Zeus at <a href="/wiki/Dodona" title="Dodona">Dodona</a> in <a href="/wiki/Epirus" title="Epirus">Epirus</a>, where there is evidence of religious activity from the second millennium BC onward, centered on a sacred oak. When the <i><a href="/wiki/Odyssey" title="Odyssey">Odyssey</a></i> was composed (circa 750 BC), divination was done there by barefoot priests called <i>Selloi</i>, who lay on the ground and observed the rustling of the leaves and branches.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[99]</a></sup> By the time <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> wrote about Dodona, female priestesses called <a href="/wiki/Peleiades" title="Peleiades">peleiades</a> ("doves") had replaced the male priests.\n</p><p>Zeus\'s consort at Dodona was not <a href="/wiki/Hera" title="Hera">Hera</a>, but the goddess <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dione_(Titaness/Oceanid)" title="Dione (Titaness/Oceanid)">Dione</a> &#8212; whose name is a feminine form of "Zeus". Her status as a <a href="/wiki/Titan_(mythology)" title="Titan (mythology)">titaness</a> suggests to some that she may have been a more powerful pre-Hellenic deity, and perhaps the original occupant of the oracle.\n</p>\n<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="The_Oracle_at_Siwa">The Oracle at Siwa</span></h4>\n<p>The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Oracle_of_Ammon" title="Oracle of Ammon">oracle of Ammon</a> at the <a href="/wiki/Siwa_Oasis" title="Siwa Oasis">Siwa Oasis</a> in the Western Desert of <a href="/wiki/Egypt" title="Egypt">Egypt</a> did not lie within the bounds of the Greek world before <a href="/wiki/Alexander_the_Great" title="Alexander the Great">Alexander</a>\'s day, but it already loomed large in the Greek mind during the archaic era: <a href="/wiki/Herodotus" title="Herodotus">Herodotus</a> mentions consultations with Zeus Ammon in his account of the <a href="/wiki/Greco-Persian_Wars" title="Greco-Persian Wars">Persian War</a>. Zeus Ammon was especially favored at <a href="/wiki/Sparta" title="Sparta">Sparta</a>, where a temple to him existed by the time of the <a href="/wiki/Peloponnesian_War" title="Peloponnesian War">Peloponnesian War</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-100"><a href="#cite_note-100">[100]</a></sup>\n</p><p>After Alexander made a trek into the desert to consult the oracle at Siwa, the figure arose in the Hellenistic imagination of a <a href="/wiki/Libyan_Sibyl" title="Libyan Sibyl">Libyan Sibyl</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Zeus_and_foreign_gods">Zeus and foreign gods</span></h2>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:402px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Evolution_of_Zeus_Nikephoros_on_Indo-Greek_coinage.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="426" data-file-width="1278" decoding="async" height="133" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Evolution_of_Zeus_Nikephoros_on_Indo-Greek_coinage.jpg/400px-Evolution_of_Zeus_Nikephoros_on_Indo-Greek_coinage.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Evolution_of_Zeus_Nikephoros_on_Indo-Greek_coinage.jpg/600px-Evolution_of_Zeus_Nikephoros_on_Indo-Greek_coinage.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b8/Evolution_of_Zeus_Nikephoros_on_Indo-Greek_coinage.jpg/800px-Evolution_of_Zeus_Nikephoros_on_Indo-Greek_coinage.jpg 2x" width="400"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Evolution_of_Zeus_Nikephoros_on_Indo-Greek_coinage.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Evolution of <i>Zeus Nikephoros</i> ("Zeus holding <a href="/wiki/Nike_(mythology)" title="Nike (mythology)">Nike</a>") on <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Indo-Greek" title="Indo-Greek">Indo-Greek</a> coinage: from the Classical motif of Nike handing the <a href="/wiki/Wreath" title="Wreath">wreath</a> of victory to Zeus himself (left, coin of <a href="/wiki/Heliocles_I" title="Heliocles I">Heliocles I</a> 145-130 BC), then to a baby <a href="/wiki/Elephant" title="Elephant">elephant</a> (middle, coin of <a href="/wiki/Antialcidas" title="Antialcidas">Antialcidas</a> 115-95 BC), and then to the <a href="/wiki/Dharmachakra" title="Dharmachakra">Wheel of the Law</a>, symbol of <a href="/wiki/Buddhism" title="Buddhism">Buddhism</a> (right, coin of <a href="/wiki/Menander_II" title="Menander II">Menander II</a> 90&#8211;85 BC).</div></div></div>\n<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Buddha-Vajrapani-Herakles.JPG"><img alt="Vajrap&#257;ni as Herakles or Zeus" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1068" data-file-width="983" decoding="async" height="239" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Buddha-Vajrapani-Herakles.JPG/220px-Buddha-Vajrapani-Herakles.JPG" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Buddha-Vajrapani-Herakles.JPG/330px-Buddha-Vajrapani-Herakles.JPG 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/3/3e/Buddha-Vajrapani-Herakles.JPG/440px-Buddha-Vajrapani-Herakles.JPG 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Buddha-Vajrapani-Herakles.JPG" title="Enlarge"></a></div>Zeus as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Vajrap%C4%81ni" title="Vajrap&#257;ni">Vajrap&#257;ni</a>, the protector of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Buddha" title="Buddha">Buddha</a>. 2nd century, <a href="/wiki/Greco-Buddhist_art" title="Greco-Buddhist art">Greco-Buddhist art</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-101"><a href="#cite_note-101">[101]</a></sup></div></div></div>\n<p>Zeus was identified with the <a href="/wiki/Roman_mythology" title="Roman mythology">Roman</a> god <a href="/wiki/Jupiter_(mythology)" title="Jupiter (mythology)">Jupiter</a> and associated in the syncretic classical imagination (see <i><a href="/wiki/Interpretatio_graeca" title="Interpretatio graeca">interpretatio graeca</a></i>) with various other deities, such as the <a href="/wiki/Egyptian_mythology" title="Egyptian mythology">Egyptian</a> <a href="/wiki/Amun" title="Amun">Ammon</a> and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Etruscan_mythology" title="Etruscan mythology">Etruscan</a> <a href="/wiki/Tinia" title="Tinia">Tinia</a>. He, along with <a href="/wiki/Dionysus" title="Dionysus">Dionysus</a>, absorbed the role of the chief <a href="/wiki/Phrygia" title="Phrygia">Phrygian</a> god <a href="/wiki/Sabazios" title="Sabazios">Sabazios</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Syncretism" title="Syncretism">syncretic</a> deity known in Rome as <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sabazius" title="Sabazius">Sabazius</a>. The Seleucid ruler <a href="/wiki/Antiochus_IV_Epiphanes" title="Antiochus IV Epiphanes">Antiochus IV Epiphanes</a> erected a statue of Zeus Olympios in the Judean Temple in Jerusalem.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-102"><a href="#cite_note-102">[102]</a></sup> Hellenizing Jews referred to this statue as Baal Shamen (in English, Lord of Heaven).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-103"><a href="#cite_note-103">[103]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Zeus_and_the_sun">Zeus and the sun</span></h2>\n<p>Zeus is occasionally conflated with the Hellenic <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Sun_god" title="Sun god">sun god</a>, <a href="/wiki/Helios" title="Helios">Helios</a>, who is sometimes either directly referred to as Zeus\' eye,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[104]</a></sup> or clearly implied as such. <a href="/wiki/Hesiod" title="Hesiod">Hesiod</a>, for instance, describes Zeus\'s eye as effectively the sun.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[105]</a></sup> This perception is possibly derived from earlier <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Proto-Indo-European_religion" title="Proto-Indo-European religion">Proto-Indo-European religion</a>, in which the sun is occasionally envisioned as the eye of <a href="/wiki/Dyeus" title="Dyeus">*<i>Dy&#7703;us P&#7717;<sub>a</sub>t&#7703;r</i></a> (see <a href="/wiki/Hvare-khshaeta" title="Hvare-khshaeta">Hvare-khshaeta</a>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[106]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Crete" title="Crete">Cretan</a> Zeus <a href="/wiki/Talos" title="Talos">Tallaios</a> had solar elements to his cult. "Talos" was the local equivalent of Helios.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[107]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Zeus_in_philosophy">Zeus in philosophy</span></h2>\n<p>In <a href="/wiki/Neoplatonism" title="Neoplatonism">Neoplatonism</a>, Zeus\'s relation to the gods familiar from mythology is taught as the <a href="/wiki/Demiurge" title="Demiurge">Demiurge</a> or Divine <a href="/wiki/Nous" title="Nous">Mind</a>. Specifically within <a href="/wiki/Plotinus" title="Plotinus">Plotinus</a>\'s work the <i><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Enneads" title="Enneads">Enneads</a></i><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[108]</a></sup> and the <i>Platonic Theology</i> of <a href="/wiki/Proclus" title="Proclus">Proclus</a>.\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Zeus_in_the_Bible">Zeus in the Bible</span></h2>\n<p>Zeus is mentioned in the New Testament twice, first in Acts 14:8&#8211;13: When the people living in <a href="/wiki/Lystra" title="Lystra">Lystra</a> saw the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Apostle_Paul" title="Apostle Paul">Apostle Paul</a> heal a lame man, they considered Paul and his partner <a href="/wiki/Barnabas" title="Barnabas">Barnabas</a> to be gods, identifying Paul with <a href="/wiki/Hermes" title="Hermes">Hermes</a> and Barnabas with Zeus, even trying to offer them sacrifices with the crowd. Two ancient inscriptions discovered in 1909 near Lystra testify to the worship of these two gods in that city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[109]</a></sup> One of the inscriptions refers to the "priests of Zeus", and the other mentions "Hermes Most Great"" and "Zeus the sun-god".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[110]</a></sup>\n</p><p>The second occurrence is in Acts 28:11: the name of the ship in which the prisoner Paul set sail from the island of Malta bore the <a href="/wiki/Figurehead" title="Figurehead">figurehead</a> "Sons of Zeus" aka <a href="/wiki/Castor_and_Pollux" title="Castor and Pollux">Castor and Pollux</a>.\n</p><p>The deuterocanonical book of <a href="/wiki/2_Maccabees" title="2 Maccabees">2 Maccabees</a> 6:1, 2 talks of King <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Antiochus_IV" title="Antiochus IV">Antiochus IV</a> (Epiphanes), who in his attempt to stamp out the Jewish religion, directed that the temple at Jerusalem be profaned and rededicated to Zeus (Jupiter Olympius).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[111]</a></sup>\n</p>\n<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="In_modern_culture">In modern culture</span></h2>\n<table class="box-In_popular_culture plainlinks metadata ambox ambox-content" role="presentation"><tbody><tr><td class="mbox-image"><div style="width:52px"><img alt="" data-file-height="40" data-file-width="40" decoding="async" height="40" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/40px-Ambox_important.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/60px-Ambox_important.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/b/b4/Ambox_important.svg/80px-Ambox_important.svg.png 2x" width="40"/></div></td><td class="mbox-text"><div class="mbox-text-span">This article <b>appears to contain <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:What_Wikipedia_is_not#Wikipedia_is_not_an_indiscriminate_collection_of_information" title="Wikipedia:What Wikipedia is not">trivial, minor, or unrelated</a> references to <a href=\'/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Trivia_sections#"In_popular_culture"_and_"Cultural_references"_material\' title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Trivia sections">popular culture</a></b>.<span class="hide-when-compact"> Please reorganize this content to explain the subject\'s impact on popular culture, using <a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources">references</a> to <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Identifying_reliable_sources" title="Wikipedia:Identifying reliable sources">reliable sources</a>, rather than simply listing appearances. Unsourced material may be challenged and removed.</span> <small class="date-container"><i>(<span class="date">January 2019</span>)</i></small></div></td></tr></tbody></table>\n<p>Depictions of Zeus as a bull, the form he took when abducting <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Europa_(mythology)" title="Europa (mythology)">Europa</a>, are found on the Greek 2-<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Euro_(currency)" title="Euro (currency)">euro</a> coin and on the <a href="/wiki/United_Kingdom" title="United Kingdom">United Kingdom</a> identity card for visa holders. <a href="/wiki/Mary_Beard_(classicist)" title="Mary Beard (classicist)">Mary Beard</a>, professor of Classics at <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Cambridge_University" title="Cambridge University">Cambridge University</a>, has criticised this for its apparent celebration of rape.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-112"><a href="#cite_note-112">[112]</a></sup>\n</p><p>Zeus has been portrayed by Axel Ringvall in <i>Jupiter p&#229; jorden</i>, the first known film adaption to feature Zeus; <a href="/wiki/Niall_MacGinnis" title="Niall MacGinnis">Niall MacGinnis</a> in <i><a are two major conflicting stories for Aphrodite\'s origins: <a href="/wiki/Hesiod" title="Hesiod">Hesiod</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Theogony" title="Theogony">Theogony</a></i> claims that she was born from the foam of the sea after Cronos castrated Uranus, making her Uranus\'s daughter but <a href="/wiki/Homer" title="Homer">Homer</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Iliad" title="Iliad">Iliad</a></i> has Aphrodite as the daughter of Zeus and Dione.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[9]</a></sup> A speaker in <a href="/wiki/Plato" title="Plato">Plato</a>\'s <i><a href="/wiki/Symposium_(Plato)" title="Symposium (Plato)">Symposium</a></i> offers that they were separate figures: <a href="/wiki/Aphrodite#Aphrodite_Ourania_and_Aphrodite_Pandemos" title="Aphrodite">Aphrodite Ourania and Aphrodite Pandemos</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[10]</a></sup></span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-12"><span Nilsson</a>, <i>Minoan-Mycenaean Religion, and Its Survival in Greek Religion</i> 1950:551 and notes.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-87"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-87">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"Professor <a href="/wiki/Stylianos_Alexiou" title="Stylianos Alexiou">Stylianos Alexiou</a> reminds us that there were other divine boys who survived from the religion of the pre-Hellenic period &#8212; <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Linos" title="Linos">Linos</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ploutos" title="Ploutos">Ploutos</a> and <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Dionysos" title="Dionysos">Dionysos</a> &#8212; so not all the young male deities we see depicted in Minoan works of art are necessarily Velchanos" (Castleden 1990:125</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-88"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-88">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Richard Wyatt Hutchinson, <i>Prehistoric Crete</i>, (Harmondsworth: Penguin) 1968:204, mentions that there is no classical reference to the death of Zeus (noted by Dietrich 1973:16 note 78).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-89"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-89">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">"This annually reborn god of vegetation also experienced the other parts of the vegetation cycle: holy marriage and annual death when he was thought to disappear from the earth" (Dietrich 1973:15).</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-90"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-90">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">In the founding myth of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Lycaon_(mythology)" title="Lycaon (mythology)">Lycaon</a>\'s banquet for the gods that included the flesh of a human sacrifice, perhaps one of his sons, <a href="/wiki/Nyctimus" title="Nyctimus">Nyctimus</a> or <a href="/wiki/Arcas" title="Arcas">Arcas</a>. href="/wiki/Theogony" title="Theogony">Theogony</a></i>,  of Zeus\' children by his seven wives, Athena was the first to be conceived; Zeus impregnated Metis then swallowed her (<a class="external text" href="http://www.perseus.tufts.edu/hopper/text?doc=Hes.+Th.+886" rel="nofollow">886&#8211;890</a>), later after mentioning the birth of his other children, Hesiod says that Zeus himself gave birth to Athena "from his head" (<a class="external text" href="http://data.perseus.org/citations/urn:cts:greekLit:tlg0020.tlg001.perseus-eng1:901-937" rel="nofollow">924&#8211;926</a>), see Gantz, rel="nofollow">183&#8211;200</a>, Aphrodite was born from Uranus\' severed genitals, see Gantz, pp. 99&#8211;100.</span>\n</li>\n<li id="cite_note-130"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a
